Understanding Permanence in God's Attributes
Discussion of permanence in divine attributes with sectarian views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi and Ash'ari positions on how God's attributes, especially knowledge, are permanent.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is permanent by essence, not by a separate accident of permanence.

Having addressed the objection about bodies, the author now faces a deeper question: if God's knowledge is permanent, does that mean it has an attribute of permanence subsisting in it? This lesson explains the nuanced Maturidi position and contrasts it with Ash'arism.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's new objection
The opponent notes that Maturidis say God's knowledge is permanent; if so, it would require an attribute of permanence, which seems impossible for divine attributes.
This objection attacks the consistency of Maturidi theology.
2
Understand the answer of the early Maturidis
The author says the early Maturidis did not describe God's knowledge as 'permanent' in the sense of having a separate attribute of permanence; they simply affirmed its eternity.
3
Contrast with the Ash'ari view
For al-Ash'ari, God is permanent by His attributes, so the attributes themselves are eternal without a separate permanence.
4
Learn the verifiers' position
The verifiers among Maturidis say God's knowledge is permanent by its essence (li-dhātihi), not by an accidental permanence. This resolves the objection.
This is the key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما سألوا عنه قولهم: إن الجسم لو كان باقيًا يقوم به لم يكن نفسه مقدورا للقديم؟ والجواب عنه أن نفي البقاء لم يجعل النفس مقدوراً للقديم بوصف البقاء أيضا، ونزيد عليه بأن نقول : لا يكون مقدورًا له في حال الوجود، لأن القدرة عندهم لا تتعلق بالموجود. ثم يقال لهذا السائل: لو كان المتحرك متحركاً لمعنى لم يكن الجسم مقدورًا للقديم متحركا ؟ فإن قال : القدرة على تحريكه قدرة على خلق الحركة له. قيل له: والقدرة على إبقائه، قدرة على خلق بقائه. فإن قال : إنكم تقولون : إن علم الله تعالى باق وكذا سائر صفاته، وقيام صفة البقاء بها مستحيل؟ والجواب: إنا بينا أن علم الله تعالى عند قدماء أصحابنا غير موصوف بالبقاء فلم يتوجه عليهم الإلزام، وعند الأشعري كان الله باقيًا بصفاته فكان بقاؤه بقاء لصفاته. والمحققون من أصحابنا يقولون : إن علمه تعالى للذات بقاء لنفسه فلم يكن باقيًا بدون البقاء، وقد استقصينا الكلام فيه وتقصينا عن عهدة ما يتوجه عليه من الإشكال في مسألة الصفات، والله الموفق. وإذا ثبت أن الاستطاعة التي يتعلق بها وجود الفعل عرض وهو مستحيل البقاء فنقول :

And we add: it is not an object of power for Him in the state of existence, because power, according to them, does not relate to the existent. Then it is said to this questioner: If the moving thing were moving by a meaning, would the body not be an object of power for the Eternal as moving? If he says: the power to move it is power to create motion for it, it is said to him: and the power to preserve it is power to create its permanence. If he says: You say that the knowledge of Allah is permanent, and likewise His other attributes, yet the subsistence of the attribute of permanence in them is impossible? The answer: We have shown that the knowledge of Allah, according to our early companions, is not described by permanence, so the objection does not apply to them. According to al-Ash'ari, Allah is permanent by His attributes, so His permanence is the permanence of His attributes. The verifiers among our companions say: His knowledge is permanent by its essence (li-dhātihi), so it is not permanent without permanence. We have discussed thoroughly and discharged the responsibility regarding the difficulties that arise in the matter of attributes. And Allah is the Giver of success. And if it is established that capability (al-istiṭā'a) by which the existence of the act is related is an accident and is impossible of permanence, we say:

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection regarding permanence and divine power, followed by a discussion on divine attributes. The objector argues: if a body is permanent by a permanence subsisting in it, then the body itself would not be under the power of the Eternal, since the permanence would be an additional attribute. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you ever imagine it as something that could change or fade? How does the truth of its permanent essence strengthen your trust in His promises and warnings today?
?
If God's attributes are eternal and unchanging, what does that mean for your own shifting feelings and convictions about faith? Will you anchor your certainty in His permanent nature rather than your temporary emotions?
?
The objection in this lesson stems from overcomplicating God's attributes. Are there areas in your own understanding of God where you add unnecessary complexities or doubts? How can you return to the simple truth that God's knowledge is sufficient and eternal?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The First Objection: Can the Body Be an Object of Power?
Al-Nasafi answers the first objection about the body being an object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Nasafi argues that the body itself is not an object of power for the Eternal in its state of existence, based on the nature of divine power.

Core Idea
Divine power relates to bringing into existence, not to what already exists.

This lesson examines the first part of a theological objection: if a body subsists by an attribute, does that prevent it from being an object of divine power? Al-Nasafi responds by clarifying that power does not pertain to the existent, but to its creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Notice the opponent's claim and Al-Nasafi's first key point: negation of subsistence does not imply the essence is an object of power.
2
Understand the further refinement
Al-Nasafi adds that in the state of existence, the body is not an object of power because power, according to the opponents themselves, does not relate to what exists.
This is a crucial principle in kalam.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this matter? It preserves the distinction between God's creative power and the stability of created beings.
This connects to the broader issue of divine attributes and secondary causality.

ومما سألوا عنه قولهم: إن الجسم لو كان باقيًا يقوم به لم يكن نفسه مقدورا للقديم؟ والجواب عنه أن نفي البقاء لم يجعل النفس مقدوراً للقديم بوصف البقاء أيضا، ونزيد عليه بأن نقول : لا يكون مقدورًا له في حال الوجود، لأن القدرة عندهم لا تتعلق بالموجود. ثم يقال لهذا السائل: لو كان المتحرك متحركاً لمعنى لم يكن الجسم مقدورًا للقديم متحركا ؟ فإن قال : القدرة على تحريكه قدرة على خلق الحركة له. قيل له: والقدرة على إبقائه، قدرة على خلق بقائه. فإن قال : إنكم تقولون : إن علم الله تعالى باق وكذا سائر صفاته، وقيام صفة البقاء بها مستحيل؟ والجواب: إنا بينا أن علم الله تعالى عند قدماء أصحابنا غير موصوف بالبقاء فلم يتوجه عليهم الإلزام، وعند الأشعري كان الله باقيًا بصفاته فكان بقاؤه بقاء لصفاته. والمحققون من أصحابنا يقولون : إن علمه تعالى للذات بقاء لنفسه فلم يكن باقيًا بدون البقاء، وقد استقصينا الكلام فيه وتقصينا عن عهدة ما يتوجه عليه من الإشكال في مسألة الصفات، والله الموفق.
And among what they asked about is their saying: If the body were subsisting by a meaning, then the essence itself would not be an object of power for the Eternal? The answer is that the negation of subsistence does not make the essence an object of power for the Eternal with the attribute of subsistence as well, and we add to that by saying: It is not an object of power for Him in the state of existence, because power, according to them, does not relate to the existent. Then it is said to this questioner: If the moving thing were moving by a meaning, then the body would not be an object of power for the Eternal moving? If he says: The power to move it is the power to create motion for it. It is said to him: And the power to preserve it is the power to create its preservation. If he says: You say that the knowledge of God Most High is subsisting, and likewise His other attributes, and that the subsistence of the attribute [of knowledge] by it is impossible? The answer: We have explained that the knowledge of God Most High, according to the early authorities among our companions, is not described with subsistence, so the objection does not apply to them; and according to al-Ash'ari, God was subsisting with His attributes, so His subsistence was subsistence for His attributes. The verifiers among our companions say that His knowledge, Most High, is for the Essence a subsistence for itself, so it was not subsisting without subsistence. And we have thoroughly discussed this and discharged ourselves of the responsibility for what difficulties arise concerning the issue of attributes. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 808

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. The opponents argue that if a body subsists (is permanent) by a meaning (i.e., an attribute of subsistence), then the body itself would not be an object of power (maqdur) for the Eternal. Al-Nasafi refutes this: first, the negation of subsistence (i.e., the fact that the body is not subsisting by a meaning) does not make the essence itself an object of power for the Eternal, because power relates only to nonexistent things, not to what already exists. Then he turns the argument around: if the objector claims that a moving thing is moving by a meaning (i.e., an attribute of motion), then the body would not be an object of power for the Eternal moving. The objector replies that power to move is power to create motion. Al-Nasafi replies that similarly, power to preserve is power to create preservation, so the body can be an object of power for its preservation. Another objection: the opponent says that God's knowledge is subsisting, and His other attributes likewise; attributing subsistence to them is impossible (because attributes subsist through the essence, not through another attribute). Al-Nasafi answers that according to the early Maturidis, God's knowledge is not described with subsistence—it is subsistent by itself; so the objection does not apply. According to al-Ash'ari, God Himself is subsisting with His attributes, so His subsistence is the subsistence of His attributes. And the verifiers say that God's knowledge, being identical to the Essence, is a subsistence for itself; therefore, it does not need a separate subsisting attribute. Thus, the objection is resolved. He concludes that he has thoroughly treated this issue elsewhere.

Key Terms
بقاء
قدرة
مقدور
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a body is not subsisting by an attribute, it must be directly an object of power in its existence.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that divine power relates to bringing things into existence, not to the already existing entity; the body's existence is a result of power, but its continued stay is not directly under power according to this argument.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafi, why is the body not an object of power for the Eternal in the state of existence?
A) Because the body does not subsist
B) Because power does not relate to the existent
C) Because the body is eternal
D) Because the Eternal has no power over bodies

Key Takeaway
Divine power is about bringing into existence, not about things that already exist; thus, an existing body's subsistence does not affect its status as an object of power for its creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If divine power is only about bringing things into existence and not about sustaining them, what does that mean for your daily reliance on God? Are you more concerned with your existence or your continued existence? How does this shift your prayer and gratitude?
?
The objection assumed that if something subsists by an attribute, it cannot be under God's power. Have you ever felt that your own good deeds or states (iman, patience) are sustained by your own effort, making you less dependent on God? How does Al-Nasafi's answer that power does not relate to the existent correct that assumption?
?
Al-Nasafi says power does not relate to the existent. If that is true, then your very being right now is not an object of God's power in the same way it was at creation. Does this make you feel less secure in God's control? Or does it highlight that your existence is a continuous gift from the One who creates anew? Examine your heart: do you truly live as if every moment is a new creation from God?

The Second Objection and Answer: Power to Create Subsistence
Al-Nasafi responds to the second objection and defends the consistency of divine power and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi refutes the second objection by showing that power over a body includes power to create its subsistence, and how the issue of God's attributes being subsisting is resolved.

Core Idea
Power to preserve is power to create preservation; divine attributes are either not described with subsistence or are subsistent by themselves.

This lesson continues the dialectic: the opponent raises a parallel argument about motion and then challenges the notion of God's knowledge being subsisting. Al-Nasafi answers by equating power to move with power to create motion, and power to preserve with power to create preservation. He then addresses the status of God's attributes, summarizing three positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogical argument about motion
Read from 'ثم يقال' to 'قدرة على خلق بقائه'. Notice how Al-Nasafi uses an analogy to turn the objection back on the opponent.
2
Analyze the opponent's reply and Al-Nasafi's response
The opponent says power to move is power to create motion. Al-Nasafi responds by saying power to preserve is power to create preservation. This shows consistency.
3
Understand the objection about God's knowledge
The opponent argues that if God's knowledge is subsisting (like the body), then it cannot be an object of power. Al-Nasafi presents three different positions among theologians to show the objection does not apply.
This part is more advanced and touches on the nature of divine attributes.
4
Summarize the three answers
Al-Nasafi mentions the early Maturidi view (knowledge not described with subsistence), the Ash'ari view (God subsisting with His attributes), and the view of the verifiers (knowledge is subsistence for itself). Each avoids the problem.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's knowledge is subsisting, it must itself be an object of power or require a separate attribute of subsistence.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that, according to different schools, either knowledge is not described with subsistence, or it is subsistent through God's own subsistence, or it is subsistence for itself—thus no infinite regress.

How does Al-Nasafi argue that power to preserve a body relates to the body?
A) Power to preserve is power to create the body itself
B) Power to preserve is power to create preservation for the body
C) Power to preserve is the same as power to move
D) Power to preserve is not needed because the body subsists by itself

Key Takeaway
The ability to preserve a body is the power to create its preservation; the nature of divine attributes, particularly knowledge, is explained in different ways to avoid the objection of requiring a separate subsistence attribute.

?

?
The passage presents different theological explanations for how God's attributes subsist. Does it unsettle you that the early scholars did not have a unified answer? How does that affect your trust in the theological tradition and your own search for certainty?
?
If God's knowledge is self-subsisting, then He knows you completely without any need for external input. Do you live as though you are truly known in this way, or do you try to hide parts of yourself? What would change if you fully internalized that God's knowledge of you is not dependent on your actions or appearances?

The Impossibility of Power Without Present Action
Nasafi exposes the absurdity of power preceding action without connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that power can precede action without being present leads to absurdity and that denying power altogether would be more logical.

Core Idea
If power is not needed at the time of action, then power is meaningless.

Imam Nasafi begins by exposing the logical flaw in the Mu'tazilite position that power (qudra) can exist before an action without being present when the action occurs. He shows that if power has no connection to the action and the action must occur in its absence, then it would be more reasonable to deny power entirely. This sets up the need for power to be simultaneous with action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the passage and identify the claim that power exists before the action without connection.
2
Trace the logical consequence
Follow the reasoning: if power is unrelated and action occurs without it, then power is unnecessary.
3
Understand the conclusion
See why denying power altogether is more logical than affirming a disconnected power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجودها سابقة على الفعل ولا تعلق له بها ويجب وجوده مع عدمها؟ فقولوا لا. فإذا قدرة البتة إذ لا يقول بوجودها من يقول إلا لضرورة حصول الفعل بها كان يستحيل وجوده بها، ويجب وجوده مع عدمها أو ثبوت ضدها، كان القول بنفيها أصلاً أحق وأصوب. ثم نقول: لما كانت القدرة المحدثة ناقصة ولم يكن بد من وجود الآلات والأسباب لتكمله هي، إذ الآلة أعدت لتكميل القدرة الناقصة، ولهذا يستحيل أن يكون الله تعالى فاعلاً بالآلة؛ لأن قدرته كاملة غير ناقصة فكانت مستغنية عما يكمل به، ثم لو جاز وجود الفعل مع انعدام حقيقة القدرة جاز أيضا مع انعدام ما أعد تتميمًا لها فيقبض الإنسان حال عدم اليد، ويمشي حال عدم الرجل، ويقطع حال عدم السكين، وحيث استحال ذلك وإن كانت هذه الآلات موجودة قبل ذلك بلا فصل فكذا في حقيقة القدرة. وفساد هذا مما لا يخفى على من رزق أدنى لب، فكذا هذا. والذي يقرر ذلك: أن القدرة لو لم يكن بها فعل [؟] وهي موجودة ويكون بها [؟] وهي غير موجودة فيكون سبب وجود الفعل عدم القدرة لا وجودها، فيصير القول به قولا بوجود الفعل لعدم القدرة، فيكون الفعل دليلاً على أن ليس الفاعل بقادر ولا على أنه قادر، وبالفعل استدلوا على أن الله تعالى قادر، فبطل موضع الاستدلال بالشاهد ووقع على العكس، وكان ينبغي أن يستدل بوجود الفعل على أن الفاعل ليس بقادر، وفيه بطلان القول بالتوحيد، ولأن القدرة إذا كانت لا تنفع وهي موجودة، فوجودها وقت الوجود وعدمها بمنزلة كانت أولى أن لا تنفع وهي معدومة، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالفعل من غير القدرة وإهدارها، وهذا محال.
Because its existence precedes the action and it has no connection to it, and it must exist with its non-existence? So say no. Then absolutely no power, since no one says it exists except for the necessity of the action occurring through it — it would be impossible for it to exist through it, and it must exist with its non-existence or the affirmation of its opposite, so denying it entirely is more correct and proper. Then we say: Since originated power is deficient and there is no alternative but to have instruments and causes to complete it — for the instrument is prepared to complete the deficient power — therefore it is impossible for God Almighty to act through an instrument because His power is complete, not deficient, so it is independent of what completes it. Then if the existence of an action were permissible with the non-existence of the reality of power, it would also be permissible with the non-existence of what is prepared as a completion for it: a person would grasp without a hand, walk without a leg, cut without a knife. And since that is impossible even though these instruments existed immediately before, so too is it impossible regarding the reality of power. The corruption of this is not hidden from one who has been granted the least intellect, so likewise this. What establishes it: that if power were such that no action occurs through it while it exists, and an action occurs through it while it does not exist, then the cause of the action's existence would be the non-existence of power, not its existence. Thus this statement becomes a statement that the action exists due to the non-existence of power. Then the action would be evidence that the agent is not powerful, and not that he is powerful. Yet they inferred from the action that God Almighty is powerful, so the basis of inference by analogy to the witnessed is invalidated and turned opposite. Rather one should infer from the existence of the action that the agent is not powerful. In this is the invalidation of the statement of tawhid. And because if power is of no benefit when it exists, then its existence at the time of existence and its non-existence are equivalent; it is more fitting that it be of no benefit when it is non-existent. In that is the necessary consequence of affirming action without power and nullifying it, and this is impossible.
Source pages p. 810

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that power (qudra) can exist before an action without being present at the time of the action. The author unfolds the argument in several steps. First, he highlights the absurdity: if power precedes action without relation, and the action must occur with its non-existence or opposite, then denying power altogether is more reasonable. Second, he draws an analogy: originated power is deficient and requires instruments; if action could occur without the reality of power, it could also occur without instruments, leading to absurd consequences like grasping without a hand. Third, he demonstrates that if action occurs through power when nonexistent, then the cause of action is the non-existence of power, making the action evidence that the agent is not powerful, contradicting the proof of God's power and tawhid. Finally, he argues that if power is useless when existing, nonexisting power is even more useless, so affirming action without power is impossible.

Key Terms
قدرة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can cause an action even if it is not present at the time of the action.
Correction: If power is absent when the action occurs, then the action cannot be attributed to that power; causality requires simultaneous presence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if power precedes the action without connection, what would be the more correct and proper position?
A) Deny power entirely.
B) Affirm that power is eternal.
C) Claim power is identical to the action.
D) Say power is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
Power must be present at the time of action; otherwise, it is irrelevant and should be denied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you rely on God's power as if it were a past event that has no bearing on your present? Where are you acting as though your own power or circumstances are sufficient right now, disconnected from the Creator's current power?
?
If you truly believed that God's power must be present at every moment for any action to occur, how would that change the way you begin your day, your work, or even your next breath? What specific habit would you change to acknowledge this reliance?
?
The passage argues that denying power altogether is more rational than affirming a power that has no effect. Are there areas in your faith where you affirm a truth about God but live as if it makes no practical difference? Will you repent of that disconnect and ask Him to make His power real in your life today?

Analogy of Instruments to the Necessity of Power
Nasafi analogizes power to instruments to show simultaneity is required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between power and physical instruments proves that power must be present at the time of action.

Core Idea
Just as an action cannot occur without a hand, it cannot occur without power.

Imam Nasafi uses a powerful analogy to demonstrate the necessity of power being present at the time of action. He explains that originated power is deficient and requires instruments to complete it. If an action could occur without power, it could also occur without instruments like hands or knives, which is absurd. Therefore, power must be present for the action.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need for instruments
Read how originated power is deficient and needs instruments to complete it.
2
Consider the conditional
If action could occur without power, it could also occur without instruments.
3
Reflect on the absurd examples
Grasping without hand, walking without leg, cutting without knife are impossible; so is action without power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Because its existence precedes the action and it has no connection to it, and it must exist with its non-existence? So say no. Then absolutely no power, since no one says it exists except for the necessity of the action occurring through it — it would be impossible for it to exist through it, and it must exist with its non-existence or the affirmation of its opposite, so denying it entirely is more correct and proper. Then we say: Since originated power is deficient and there is no alternative but to have instruments and causes to complete it — for the instrument is prepared to complete the deficient power — therefore it is impossible for God Almighty to act through an instrument because His power is complete, not deficient, so it is independent of what completes it. Then if the existence of an action were permissible with the non-existence of the reality of power, it would also be permissible with the non-existence of what is prepared as a completion for it: a person would grasp without a hand, walk without a leg, cut without a knife. And since that is impossible even though these instruments existed immediately before, so too is it impossible regarding the reality of power. The corruption of this is not hidden from one who has been granted the least intellect, so likewise this. What establishes it: that if power were such that no action occurs through it while it exists, and an action occurs through it while it does not exist, then the cause of the action's existence would be the non-existence of power, not its existence. Thus this statement becomes a statement that the action exists due to the non-existence of power. Then the action would be evidence that the agent is not powerful, and not that he is powerful. Yet they inferred from the action that God Almighty is powerful, so the basis of inference by analogy to the witnessed is invalidated and turned opposite. Rather one should infer from the existence of the action that the agent is not powerful. In this is the invalidation of the statement of tawhid. And because if power is of no benefit when it exists, then its existence at the time of existence and its non-existence are equivalent; it is more fitting that it be of no benefit when it is non-existent. In that is the necessary consequence of affirming action without power and nullifying it, and this is impossible.
Source pages p. 810

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that power (qudra) can exist before an action without being present at the time of the action. The author unfolds the argument in several steps. First, he highlights the absurdity: if power precedes action without relation, and the action must occur with its non-existence or opposite, then denying power altogether is more reasonable. Second, he draws an analogy: originated power is deficient and requires instruments; if action could occur without the reality of power, it could also occur without instruments, leading to absurd consequences like grasping without a hand. Third, he demonstrates that if action occurs through power when nonexistent, then the cause of action is the non-existence of power, making the action evidence that the agent is not powerful, contradicting the proof of God's power and tawhid. Finally, he argues that if power is useless when existing, nonexisting power is even more useless, so affirming action without power is impossible.

Key Terms
قدرة
فعل
آلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Actions can occur without power as long as the agent previously had the power.
Correction: Just as an action cannot occur without a hand, it cannot occur without power at that moment.

Checkpoint
What impossible scenario does Nasafi use to illustrate the absurdity of allowing action without power?
A) Speaking without a tongue.
B) Grasping without a hand.
C) Thinking without a brain.
D) Seeing without eyes.

Key Takeaway
The analogy to instruments shows that power must be present for action, just as a hand is needed for grasping.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that without power, an action is as impossible as grasping without a hand. When you fall short in your obedience, do you truly feel the weight of your dependency on God's granting of power at that very moment? How can you train yourself to beg for His power each time you intend to do good, rather than relying on past resolve?
?
The analogy shows that using an instrument does not make God’s power deficient, but rather it is we who need instruments. In what areas of your worship have you become so attached to the 'instruments' (rituals, places, times) that you forget the true Source of power? Are you willing to let go of these when they are absent, or do you secretly believe they are indispensable?
?
If it is absurd to claim you can cut without a knife, how much more absurd is it to claim you can live righteously without God's power present at every instant? Pause and consider: are you living as though you have a 'reserve' of spiritual power from yesterday, or do you acknowledge that your next good deed is utterly impossible without His immediate help? What will you do to remind yourself of this before your next action?

How Absent Power Undermines the Proof of Tawhid
Nasafi shows that absent power makes action evidence of powerless, contradicting tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite view of power undermines the proof of God's power and the principle of tawhid.

Core Idea
If action exists without power, then action proves the agent is not powerful — contradicting tawhid.

Imam Nasafi delivers the final blow to the Mu'tazilite position by showing its logical consequence: if an action occurs while power is absent, then the action would be evidence that the agent lacks power. This would invalidate the standard inference from creation to God's power, and even lead to the denial of tawhid. He also argues that if power is useless when present, it is even more useless when absent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical
Imagine power does not cause action when present, but action occurs when power is absent.
2
Grasp the reversal of inference
Normally action indicates a powerful agent; here it would indicate a powerless agent.
3
Reflect on the consequence
This would undermine the proof of God's power and tawhid itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجودها سابقة على الفعل ولا تعلق له بها ويجب وجوده مع عدمها؟ فقولوا لا. فإذا قدرة البتة إذ لا يقول بوجودها من يقول إلا لضرورة حصول الفعل بها كان يستحيل وجوده بها، ويجب وجوده مع عدمها أو ثبوت ضدها، كان القول بنفيها أصلاً أحق وأصوب. ثم نقول: لما كانت القدرة المحدثة ناقصة ولم يكن بد من وجود الآلات والأسباب لتكمله هي، إذ الآلة أعدت لتكميل القدرة الناقصة، ولهذا يستحيل أن يكون الله تعالى فاعلاً بالآلة؛ لأن قدرته كاملة غير ناقصة فكانت مستغنية عما يكمل به، ثم لو جاز وجود الفعل مع انعدام حقيقة القدرة جاز أيضا مع انعدام ما أعد تتميمًا لها فيقبض الإنسان حال عدم اليد، ويمشي حال عدم الرجل، ويقطع حال عدم السكين، وحيث استحال ذلك وإن كانت هذه الآلات موجودة قبل ذلك بلا فصل فكذا في حقيقة القدرة. وفساد هذا مما لا يخفى على من رزق أدنى لب، فكذا هذا. والذي يقرر ذلك: أن القدرة لو لم يكن بها فعل [؟] وهي موجودة ويكون بها [؟] وهي غير موجودة فيكون سبب وجود الفعل عدم القدرة لا وجودها، فيصير القول به قولا بوجود الفعل لعدم القدرة، فيكون الفعل دليلاً على أن ليس الفاعل بقادر ولا على أنه قادر، وبالفعل استدلوا على أن الله تعالى قادر، فبطل موضع الاستدلال بالشاهد ووقع على العكس، وكان ينبغي أن يستدل بوجود الفعل على أن الفاعل ليس بقادر، وفيه بطلان القول بالتوحيد، ولأن القدرة إذا كانت لا تنفع وهي موجودة، فوجودها وقت الوجود وعدمها بمنزلة كانت أولى أن لا تنفع وهي معدومة، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالفعل من غير القدرة وإهدارها، وهذا محال.

Proof of Divine Power from Temporal Impossibility
The author presents a proof from opponents' agreement on temporal impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's proof that an act cannot be caused by a power that is absent at the time of the act, based on the opponents' own concession.

Core Idea
If an act cannot occur by a power preceding it by many times, it cannot by one time either.

In this passage, the author defends his position on divine power by building on a concession from his opponents. They agree that an act cannot exist by a power that preceded it by a long interval. The author then extends that reasoning to even a single moment, using a striking analogy to show that the length of absence is irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument flow. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice the conditional structure at the beginning and the analogy at the end.
2
Identify the opponents' concession
The author says the opponents agreed that an act cannot exist by a power that preceded it by many times. This is the foundation of the proof.
3
Understand the analogical reasoning
The author uses the analogy of striking with a hand that ceased to exist. Consider why the length of time after the hand's disappearance does not matter.
The key point is the absence of the power at the time of the act.
4
Connect the analogy to the proof
Realize that the same logic applies to the power: if the power is absent at the moment of the act, the act cannot occur, regardless of how short the absence is.
This shows the opponents cannot escape by claiming a short interval makes a difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم: إذا كانت هذه القدرة الثابتة لفعل آخر لا لهذا الفعل ويستحيل تعلق الفعل المقارن لها بها بل تعلقه بالقدرة الأولى وحصوله بها لا بهذه، وكان وجود هذه القدرة في حق الفعل وعدمها بمنزلة أن القدرة على فعل لا يوجب زوال العجز عن فعل آخر، ولزمكم جميع ما ألزمناكم، ولا تخلص لكم بوجه من الوجوه. والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن الخصوم ساعدونا على استحالة وجود الفعل بقدرة متقدمة عليه بأوقات كثيرة، فكذا يستحيل وجوده بقدرة متقدمة عليه بوقت واحد لاستوائهما في العدم وقت الفعل، ألا ترى أن البطش بيد كانت ثم انعدمت محال وإن اتصل وجود الفعل بزمان وجود اليد إذا كانت منعدمة وقت الفعل. كما أنه محال في زمان وجد بعد زمان انعدام اليد بمدة مديدة لاستوائهما في عدم اليد زمان البطش فكذا هذا
It was said to them: if this power established for another act, not for this act, and it is impossible for the concomitant act to be attached to it, rather its attachment is to the first power and its occurrence by it, not by this one, and the existence of this power with respect to the act and its non-existence are like that power over an act does not necessitate removal of inability from another act, then all that we have required you is necessary upon you, and you have no escape in any way. And what indicates the correctness of what we have gone to is that the opponents agreed with us on the impossibility of the existence of an act by a power that preceded it by many times, so likewise it is impossible for its existence by a power that preceded it by one time, because they are equal in non-existence at the time of the act. Do you not see that striking with a hand that existed then ceased is impossible, even if the existence of the act is connected to the time of the existence of the hand if it is non-existent at the time of the act, just as it is impossible at a time that came after the time of the non-existence of the hand by a long period because they are equal in the non-existence of the hand at the time of the striking? So it is the same.
Source pages p. 814

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the author's position and a proof for it. It opens by summarizing the opponents' concession: if a power exists for one act and not another, and the act cannot occur by that second power, then the opponents are forced into the author's position. Then the author gives his proof: the opponents agree that an act cannot occur by a power that preceded it by many times (since that power is absent at the time of the act). So equally, it cannot occur by a power that preceded it by just one time, because in both cases the power is non-existent at the time of the act. He illustrates with the analogy of striking with a hand: if the hand ceases to exist before the striking, the striking is impossible even if the act occurs at the very moment the hand ceases. Similarly, it is impossible if the hand vanished long before. The two cases are equal in the hand's non-existence at the time of striking. Therefore, the same reasoning applies to power and act.

Correction: This passage concerns divine power and its relation to created acts; the hand analogy is purely illustrative.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an act by a power absent at its time is independent of the length of the absence, so a single moment's absence suffices to break the causal link.

Two Meanings of Qudra
Al-Nasafi identifies two uses of 'qudra' in the opponent's doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'qudra' in the opponent's framework refers to two distinct realities: the soundness of causes and instruments, and the true essential power that causes the act.

Core Idea
'Qudra' is not one thing but two: conditional power and essential power.

Al-Nasafi begins his refutation by exposing a fundamental confusion. His opponent treats 'qudra' as a single concept, but according to their own doctrine, it actually refers to two distinct realities. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the three states of the human agent that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening of the refutation
Read the Arabic passage once. Al-Nasafi starts by stating that the opponent's argument arises from ignorance of their own doctrine and of the reality of possibility.
Notice the phrase 'صدر عن الجهل بمذاهب الخصوم'.
2
Identify the two meanings of qudra
The passage explicitly lists two things for which the opponent uses the word 'qudra': (1) soundness of causes and health of instruments, and (2) the unknown meaning which is the reality of power.
The first is conditional, the second is essential.
3
Understand why the second meaning is called 'unknown'
Al-Nasafi calls the second meaning 'المعنى المجهول للفعل' because it is the intrinsic causal power that is not directly observable.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This distinction sets the stage for the three states of the human agent, where these two powers may be present or absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأقول في الكشف عن هذا التمويه – وبالله التوفيق:
إن هذا الكلام مع هذا التطويل والتهويل صدر عن الجهل بمذاهب الخصوم وجعل ما هو عند خصمه اسم لشيئين متغايرين اسمًا لشيء واحد، وعن الجهل بحقيقة الإمكان، وذلك لأن عند خصمه القدرة اسم لشيئين:
أحدهما : سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات، وهي تسمى قدرة لحدوث القدرة فيها في المعتاد عند قصد الفعل وامتناع حدوثها عند فواتها.
والثاني: المعنى المجهول للفعل وهو حقيقة القدرة، وهو الذي نتكلم فيه بعد وقبل. وللإنسان أحوال ثلاثة حالة عدم الأسباب وفساد الآلات أو عدمها، وكان الفعل فيها ممتنعاً لا لفساد الآلات بل لانعدام القدرة، إلا أن وجود القدرة مع فوات الأسباب ممتنع لامتناع وجود القدرة. وحالة وجود سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات وعدم حقيقة القدرة وكان الفعل فيه ممكنا إذ لو قصد الفعل لحصلت له حقيقة القدرة وإن لم يقصد لم يحصل. وحالة وجود سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات ووجود القدرة التي اختلفنا في وجودها وقت الفعل وعدمها وكان الفعل فيها موجودًا فكانت حالة وجود الفعل حالة وجود القدرتين جميعًا، وحالة إمكانه حالة إحدى القدرتين وهي قدرة الأسباب والآلات لا غير، وحالة امتناعه حالة فوات القدرتين جميعًا.
I say in uncovering this obfuscation – and with God is success: This speech, despite its length and exaggeration, arises from ignorance of the doctrines of the opponents and making what for the opponent is a name for two distinct things into a name for one thing, and from ignorance of the reality of possibility. That is because, according to his opponent, 'qudra' (power) is a name for two things: one of them is the soundness of causes and the health of instruments, and it is called power due to the occurrence of power in them customarily upon intending the act and the prevention of its occurrence upon their loss. The second is the unknown meaning for the act, which is the reality of power, and it is what we discuss afterwards and before. And for the human there are three states: a state of absence of causes and corruption or absence of instruments, and in it the act is impossible not due to the corruption of instruments but due to the non-existence of power, except that existence of power with the loss of causes is impossible due to the impossibility of the existence of power. And a state of existence of soundness of causes and health of instruments and absence of the reality of power, and the act in it is possible, for if he intended the act he would obtain the reality of power, and if he did not intend it would not be obtained. And a state of existence of soundness of causes and health of instruments and existence of the power over whose existence at the time of the act we differ, and the act in it exists, so the state of existence of the act is a state of existence of both powers together, and the state of its possibility is a state of one of the two powers, namely the power of causes and instruments only, and the state of its impossibility is a state of loss of both powers together.
Source pages p. 821

Commentary
This passage is a refutation where al-Nasafi identifies two confusions in his opponent's argument: ignorance of the opponent's own doctrine (which uses 'qudra' for two distinct things) and ignorance of the reality of imkan (possibility). He then clarifies that 'qudra' has two meanings: (1) the soundness of causes and health of instruments (a conditional/attributed power), and (2) the true essential power (the reality that causes the act). These two are distinct. This leads to three states for the human agent: (a) absence or corruption of instruments – here the act is impossible because there is no power at all (neither type can exist); (b) instruments exist but the true power is absent – here the act is possible: if the agent intends the act, God creates the true power; (c) both instruments and true power exist – then the act actually occurs. Thus the state of existence of the act is the simultaneous existence of both powers; the state of possibility is the existence of only the power of causes; the state of impossibility is the loss of both. This analysis refutes the opponent's claim of an intermediate state.

قدرة
سلامة الأسباب
صحة الآلات
حقيقة القدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'qudra' is a single undifferentiated power.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that his opponent's own usage distinguishes between conditional power (soundness of causes) and essential power (the reality of power).

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, how many meanings does the term 'qudra' have for his opponent?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The term 'qudra' in the opponent's framework refers to two distinct realities: the conditional power of causes and the essential power of the act.

?

?
Al-Nasafi calls the essential power 'the unknown meaning'. Are you comfortable knowing that the very power by which you act is ultimately beyond your full understanding? How does that recognition challenge your self-sufficiency or pride?

intermediate
7 min
Three States of the Human Agent
Al-Nasafi enumerates three states of the human agent based on two powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three states of the human agent based on the presence or absence of the two types of qudra.

Core Idea
The human agent has three states: impossible, possible, or actual — depending on which powers exist.

Al-Nasafi now applies the distinction between the two meanings of qudra to analyze the human agent's condition. He identifies three states, each corresponding to a different combination of the two powers. This analysis refutes the opponent's claim of an intermediate state by showing that only three logical states exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the three states
Read the passage describing the three states of the human agent. Each state is defined by the presence or absence of the two powers.
2
Identify the first state
The first state: absence of causes and instruments. Here the act is impossible because no power exists at all. Note that even the essential power cannot exist without causes.
This is the state of impossibility.
3
Identify the second state
The second state: causes and instruments are sound, but the essential power is absent. Here the act is possible: if the agent intends it, the essential power will be created.
This is the state of possibility.
4
Identify the third state
The third state: both causes and essential power exist. Here the act actually occurs. Al-Nasafi concludes that the state of existence of the act is when both powers are present; the state of possibility is when only the power of causes exists; the state of impossibility is when both are absent.
This demonstrates that there is no intermediate state beyond these three.
5
Understand the refutation
The three states cover all possibilities, refuting any claim of an act being possible without the essential power or impossible despite the essential power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأقول في الكشف عن هذا التمويه – وبالله التوفيق:
إن هذا الكلام مع هذا التطويل والتهويل صدر عن الجهل بمذاهب الخصوم وجعل ما هو عند خصمه اسم لشيئين متغايرين اسمًا لشيء واحد، وعن الجهل بحقيقة الإمكان، وذلك لأن عند خصمه القدرة اسم لشيئين:
أحدهما : سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات، وهي تسمى قدرة لحدوث القدرة فيها في المعتاد عند قصد الفعل وامتناع حدوثها عند فواتها.
والثاني: المعنى المجهول للفعل وهو حقيقة القدرة، وهو الذي نتكلم فيه بعد وقبل. وللإنسان أحوال ثلاثة حالة عدم الأسباب وفساد الآلات أو عدمها، وكان الفعل فيها ممتنعاً لا لفساد الآلات بل لانعدام القدرة، إلا أن وجود القدرة مع فوات الأسباب ممتنع لامتناع وجود القدرة. وحالة وجود سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات وعدم حقيقة القدرة وكان الفعل فيه ممكنا إذ لو قصد الفعل لحصلت له حقيقة القدرة وإن لم يقصد لم يحصل. وحالة وجود سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات ووجود القدرة التي اختلفنا في وجودها وقت الفعل وعدمها وكان الفعل فيها موجودًا فكانت حالة وجود الفعل حالة وجود القدرتين جميعًا، وحالة إمكانه حالة إحدى القدرتين وهي قدرة الأسباب والآلات لا غير، وحالة امتناعه حالة فوات القدرتين جميعًا.
I say in uncovering this obfuscation – and with God is success: This speech, despite its length and exaggeration, arises from ignorance of the doctrines of the opponents and making what for the opponent is a name for two distinct things into a name for one thing, and from ignorance of the reality of possibility. That is because, according to his opponent, 'qudra' (power) is a name for two things: one of them is the soundness of causes and the health of instruments, and it is called power due to the occurrence of power in them customarily upon intending the act and the prevention of its occurrence upon their loss. The second is the unknown meaning for the act, which is the reality of power, and it is what we discuss afterwards and before. And for the human there are three states: a state of absence of causes and corruption or absence of instruments, and in it the act is impossible not due to the corruption of instruments but due to the non-existence of power, except that existence of power with the loss of causes is impossible due to the impossibility of the existence of power. And a state of existence of soundness of causes and health of instruments and absence of the reality of power, and the act in it is possible, for if he intended the act he would obtain the reality of power, and if he did not intend it would not be obtained. And a state of existence of soundness of causes and health of instruments and existence of the power over whose existence at the time of the act we differ, and the act in it exists, so the state of existence of the act is a state of existence of both powers together, and the state of its possibility is a state of one of the two powers, namely the power of causes and instruments only, and the state of its impossibility is a state of loss of both powers together.
Source pages p. 821

Commentary
This passage is a refutation where al-Nasafi identifies two confusions in his opponent's argument: ignorance of the opponent's own doctrine (which uses 'qudra' for two distinct things) and ignorance of the reality of imkan (possibility). He then clarifies that 'qudra' has two meanings: (1) the soundness of causes and health of instruments (a conditional/attributed power), and (2) the true essential power (the reality that causes the act). These two are distinct. This leads to three states for the human agent: (a) absence or corruption of instruments – here the act is impossible because there is no power at all (neither type can exist); (b) instruments exist but the true power is absent – here the act is possible: if the agent intends the act, God creates the true power; (c) both instruments and true power exist – then the act actually occurs. Thus the state of existence of the act is the simultaneous existence of both powers; the state of possibility is the existence of only the power of causes; the state of impossibility is the loss of both. This analysis refutes the opponent's claim of an intermediate state.

صحة الآلات
حقيقة القدرة
الإمكان
أحوال ثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that an act can be possible without the presence of sound causes and instruments.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that possibility requires the power of causes to be present; without it, the act is impossible.

Checkpoint
In which of the three states does the act actually exist according to al-Nasafi?
A) When causes are absent
B) When only the power of causes exists
C) When both powers exist
D) When only the essential power exists

Key Takeaway
The human agent has only three possible states: act impossible (both powers absent), act possible (only power of causes present), or act actual (both powers present).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you had all the means to do something good but did not do it. According to this lesson, you were in the state of possibility. What was missing? What intention did you fail to form, and what does that reveal about your reliance on God's creation of power within you? How will you respond next time you find yourself in that state?
?
When you commit a sin, you might blame circumstances or your own weakness. But this lesson teaches that the act only occurs when both external causes and essential power from God are present. How does that reshape your understanding of personal responsibility? What will you do to ensure you seek God's help in both preparing causes and intending good?
?
In which state do you live most of your day: impossibility (lack of means), possibility (means present but no intention), or actuality (both present)? Be honest. If you spend most of your time in possibility, what keeps you from intending and acting? What concrete step can you take today to move from possibility to actuality in a matter that pleases Allah?

Two Types of Incapacity and Their Relationship to Power
Al-Nasafi defines incapacity and distinguishes two types based on the loss of different powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's definition of incapacity as the direct opposite of power and his distinction between two types based on the loss of either the soundness of causes or the reality of power itself.

Core Idea
Incapacity is not always permanent; it depends on which power is lost.

Al-Nasafi begins by establishing incapacity as the direct opposite of power, with no intermediate state. He then demonstrates that incapacity can arise from the loss of either the physical causes or the true power itself, leading to two distinct types. This passage clarifies that not all incapacities are equal—some are temporary, others permanent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice how al-Nasafi defines incapacity and introduces the two types.
Pay attention to the example of carrying weights.
2
Identify the two types
Find where al-Nasafi explicitly says 'incapacity is of two types' and note the basis for each type: loss of causes vs. loss of true power.
The example illustrates the second type.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters in kalam: it affects whether something is considered possible or impossible.
This connects to discussions about human capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العجز يقابل القدرة، ولن يعرف العقلاء بينهما واسطة في حق من يصح اتصافه بها كالاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون في حالة البقاء، ثم إذا فاتت سلامة الأسباب والآلات ثبت العجز، وإذا فاتت حقيقة القدرة ثبت العجز أيضا على ما بينا أن الإنسان بالآلة يحمل مائة رطل ثم قد لا يقدر إلا على حمل خمسين رطلاً مع بقاء سلامة الأسباب، فهو إذا عاجز وإن لم تفت القدرة الأولى، والفعل مع العجز غير متحقق، وجاء من هذا أن العجز على نوعين: عجز لا يرجى ثبوت القدرة عقيب قصد الفعل مقارنا للفعل في غالب العادة وهو العجز الثابت عند فوت سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات. وعجز يرجى ثبوت القدرة بل تحصل القدرة عقيب وجود القصد مقارناً للفعل كالموجود المتحقق لوجوده في غالب العادة عقيب قصد الفعل مقارنا للفعل بناء للأمر على المعتاد، فالمعجوز عنه لفوت هذه القدرة يسمى ممكنا، والمعجوز عنه لفوت القدرة الأولى التي لا يرجى ثبوت القدرة الحقيقة مع فوتها يسمى.
Then incapacity (al-‘ajz) is opposed to power, and the intelligent know no intermediate between them regarding one whom it is valid to describe with them, like conjunction and separation and motion and rest in the state of remaining. Then, if the soundness of causes and instruments is lost, incapacity is established; and if the reality of power is lost, incapacity is also established, as we explained that a person with an instrument carries one hundred ratls, then may not be able to carry except fifty ratls while the soundness of causes remains, so he is then incapable even if the first power is not lost; and the act with incapacity is not actualized. And from this it follows that incapacity is of two types: an incapacity for which the establishment of power after intending the act concurrent with the act in most custom is not hoped for, and it is the incapacity established upon loss of soundness of causes and health of instruments. And an incapacity for which the establishment of power is hoped – rather power occurs after the existence of intention concurrent with the act, like the existing which is verified to exist in most custom after intending the act concurrent with the act, building the matter on custom. So that which is incapable of due to the loss of this power is called possible, and that which is incapable of due to the loss of the first power, along with which the establishment of the true power is not hoped, is called...
Source pages p. 822

Commentary
This passage defines incapacity (al-‘ajz) as the direct opposite of power (al-qudra), with no intermediate state for those who can be described by either – similar to opposites like conjunction/separation or motion/rest in the state of remaining. Al-Nasafi then explains that the loss of either type of power (the power of causes/instruments or the true power) results in incapacity. He gives an example: a person who can normally carry 100 ratls with an instrument may, due to some condition, be unable to carry 50 ratls even though the instrument is sound – this is incapacity from the loss of true power. Thus incapacity is of two types: (1) incapacity where the true power is not hoped to arise after intending the act – this happens when the causes/instruments are lost (e.g., paralysis); (2) incapacity where the true power is hoped to arise after intention and usually does – this is the state where only the power of causes exists, and the act remains possible. The object that is impossible due to the first incapacity is called 'impossible', while the object that is impossible due to the second (temporary) incapacity is called 'possible' (i.e., the act is possible if intention occurs).

القدرة

حقيقة القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incapacity is always permanent and unchanging.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that incapacity can be temporary when the true power is absent but causes remain; power can be regained with intention.

Checkpoint
How many types of incapacity does al-Nasafi identify?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
Incapacity has two forms: one from loss of causes (permanent) and one from loss of true power (temporary), and the latter allows for possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the excuse 'I can't' when the real issue was lack of intention, not true inability? How would acknowledging the possibility change your approach to that task starting tomorrow?
?
Al-Nasafi says the intelligent see no gray area between power and incapacity. In what area of your life are you pretending there is a middle ground—knowing you actually have the means but avoiding the effort? What will you do about it today?
?
If temporary incapacity still leaves the act possible, what does that say about the way you treat yourself when you fail? Will you continue to label yourself as permanently incapable, or will you see failure as a call to renew your intention? Write one specific commitment.

Refutation of Power's Persistence
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine that power persists until the action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school rejects the Mu'tazilite doctrine of the persistence of power (qudra) as an accident.

Core Idea
Accidents do not persist — power cannot linger until action.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi engages directly with the Mu'tazilite claim that power (qudra) remains after its creation until the moment of action. He argues that this idea is impossible because accidents (a'rad) cannot persist, and even if they could, it would not solve the theological problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the forceful tone and the structure of the argument: the Mu'tazilite claim, then Nasafi's two-pronged refutation.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so the final rhetorical question is left hanging. Think about what the expected answer would be.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazila assert that power exists before the action and then persists until the action occurs. This is necessary, they say, to avoid an action being performed by someone who is not powerful.
This is the doctrine of baqa' al-qudra (persistence of power).
3
Analyze Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi first invokes the established proof against the persistence of accidents in general. Then he makes a conditional argument: even if we grant persistence, it still doesn't help you. The rhetorical question implies that the state of persistence itself is problematic.
Consider why the persistence of an accident is considered impossible in Maturidi thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحمل هذا الكلام بعض المعتزلة على أن ركبوا المحال، وجوزوا بقاء القدرة وقالوا: القدرة توجد قبل الفعل، ثم تبقى إلى وقت وجود الفعل فلا يكون الفعل ثابتا ممن ليس بقادر. وقد أقمنا الدلالة على بطلان القول ببقاء الأعراض فلم يمكنهم التعلق بهذا المحال، ثم نقول : هب أن القدرة تبقى إلى وقت وجود الفعل ولكن مع هذا لا ينفعكم ذلك فإنا نقول لكم : إذا بقيت إلى وقت وجود (الفعل) أكانت حالة البقاء
And some Mu'tazila undertook this speech and invented the impossible, and permitted the persistence (baqa') of power and said: Power exists before the action, then persists until the time of the action's existence, so the action is not established from one who is not powerful. And we have established the proof of the invalidity of the doctrine of the persistence of accidents (baqa' al-a'rad), so they cannot cling to this impossibility. Then we say: Suppose power persists until the time of the action's existence, but even so this does not benefit you, for we say to you: If it persists until the time of the existence of (the action), was it in the state of persistence
Source pages p. 824

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite attempt to escape by claiming that power (qudra) persists (baqa') after its creation until the time of action. Nasafi first states that his school has already proven the invalidity of the doctrine of the persistence of accidents (baqa' al-a'rad), so this escape route is impossible. He then makes a conditional argument: even if one were to accept that power persists, it does not solve the problem. He asks a rhetorical question that likely expects a negative answer: 'If it persists until the time of the existence of the action, was it in the state of persistence?' The implication is that persistence itself is denied, and even if conceded, further difficulties arise. The passage demonstrates the Maturidi rejection of the duration of accidents, which underpins their theory of power and action.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That accidents can persist over time, so power can remain until the action.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the persistence of accidents in general has been proven false, and even if power persisted, the argument does not hold.

Checkpoint

B) The refutation of the persistence of accidents
C) The necessity of power at the time of action

Key Takeaway
Nasafi rejects the Mu'tazilite persistence of power because accidents cannot endure, and even if they could, the argument leads to an unresolved problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument hinges on the nature of accidents—things that do not endure. Are you living as if your faith, your patience, your love for Allah are accidents that need to be renewed moment by moment? What would change in your prayer if you truly believed that your very existence is renewed each instant?

Refutation of No New Meaning in Power
Refutes the objection that no new meaning changes the power's relation to the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the power's relation to the act cannot change without a new attribute, and the absurd consequences of denying this.

Core Idea
A thing's essential impossibility cannot become possibility without a new cause.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that the power (qudra) remains unchanged in the second moment, yet supposedly relates to the act. He argues that if the power is identical and was unable to relate in the first moment, it remains unable in the second. To claim otherwise leads to absurdities—like inability being able to produce an act. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening line: 'If they say: No meaning occurred in it in the second [moment] that necessitated its change from what it was in the first.' This is the objection that the power remains identical.
The objectors assume no new attribute (ma'na) arises.
2
Follow the author's reply
Notice the author's use of the principle that identical things have identical properties. Since the power was unable to relate in the first moment, it remains unable in the second.
The author states 'because it is the very thing' – emphasizing numerical identity.
3
Recognize the reductio ad absurdum
The author then shows that if the objection were true, it would permit saying that inability ('ajz) could also produce an act, which is impossible. This demonstrates the fallacy.
This is a classic kalam argument: if something impossible becomes possible without a cause, anything could happen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لم تحدث فيها في الثاني معنى أوجب تغيرها عما كانت في الأول.
قلنا: إذا كانت هي في الأول موجودة ويستحيل تعلقها بالفعل وثبوته بها يستحيل ذلك أيضا في الثاني، لأنها عين ما كان يستحيل تعلقه بالفعل ويستحيل ثبوته به والقول بوجوب تعلق شيء بشيء ووجوب ثبوت الثاني بالأول أن ذلك كان لذاته مما يستحيل تعلقه بالثاني وثبوت الثاني بالأول قول محال، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز القول بوجوب تعلق العجز بالفعل ووجوب ثبوت الفعل به في حال وإن ووجوب استحال ذلك في حال من غير حدوث معنى مغير، وهذا عين المحال. وقد أورد هذا الفصل على السيرافي بشيراز فانقطع أفحش انقطاع وأوحشه.
If they say: No meaning occurred in it in the second [moment] that necessitated its change from what it was in the first. We say: If it existed in the first and its relation to the act and the act's subsistence through it were impossible, then that is also impossible in the second, because it is the very thing whose relation to the act was impossible and whose subsistence through the act was impossible. And the statement that it necessitates the relation of something to something and the subsistence of the second through the first is that that was due to its essence—something whose relation to the second and the subsistence of the second through the first are impossible—is an impossible statement. If that were permissible, then it would be permissible to say that inability necessitates relation to the act and the act's subsistence through it in a state, while its being impossible in a state without the occurrence of a changing meaning is pure impossibility. And he presented this section to al-Sirafi in Shiraz, and he [al-Sirafi] was cut off in the ugliest and most desolate way.
Source pages p. 826

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors claim that no new meaning (ma'na) arises in the power (qudra) in the second moment to cause it to change. The author argues that since the power is identical in both moments, and it was impossible for it to be related to the act in the first moment, it must also be impossible in the second. To claim that something essentially impossible can become possible without a new cause would allow the absurd consequence that inability ('ajz) could be related to the act. The author then notes that this argument utterly silenced al-Sirafi.

Key Terms

معنى
تغير
تعلق
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a change in the power's relation to the act can occur without a new attribute or meaning.
Correction: The author shows that if the power is unchanged, its properties (including impossibility of relation) remain unchanged; allowing change without a cause leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would follow if it were permissible for power to be unchanged yet produce an act in the second moment?
A) That power is eternal.
B) That inability could also be related to an act.
C) That the act is pre-eternal.
D) That power is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that allowing an unchanged power to produce a new act would entail that inability could also produce an act, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may believe that God can do anything at any time. But according to this lesson, even God's power requires a new 'meaning' for a new act. How does this make you rethink your understanding of how God acts in your life? Will you now look for the 'new meanings' that God creates each moment?
?
The objector denied any change in the power, yet the author showed that leads to absurdity. In your own faith, are there positions you hold that, if followed logically, would lead to something absurd about Allah? What would it take for you to let go of that comfortable but contradictory belief?
?
The debater al-Sirafi was cut off – he could not answer. Imagine yourself in his place. Do you have the humility to admit when your reasoning about Allah is flawed, or do you cling to your argument despite its obvious contradiction? How will you ensure your aqeedah is consistent with the clear proofs of the scholars?

Contradiction in the Opponent's View on Agency
Shows that the opponent's view makes the agent incapable in both moments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponent's view on agency leads to a contradiction that invalidates their claim that the agent is a doer or creator.

Core Idea
The opponent's view makes the agent incapable in both moments, destroying agency.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi continues his refutation of a rival theological school by exposing a logical flaw in their account of agency. He presents two arguments showing that their position leads to the conclusion that the agent is never truly capable of acting, undermining their claim that he is a doer or creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the tone of refutation and the logical structure.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the first argument about the two moments (first and second) and the second argument about power being free of act. Understand how each undermines the opponent's view.
The first argument is summed up in 'so the sum of both states...'
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Consider why the opponent cannot consistently call the agent a 'doer' or 'creator' if their own premises lead to the agent being incapable in both moments.
The second argument strengthens the first by showing that if power can be empty once, it can always be empty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: من مذهبكم أن الفعل لا يصح من القادر في الأول، وفي الثاني لا يوصف هو بالقدرة على الفعل، فكان مجموع الحالين أنه لا يصح منه الفعل ولا يقدر عليه، فكيف تجعلونه فاعلاً بل خالقًا لقوله؟ وما لا يصح وجوده ولا قدرة عليه لا يتصور وجوده.
على أننا بينا من الكلام في جانب القدرة أن خلوها عن الفعل لو جاز وقتًا من مع وجود ذاتها لجاز أوقاتًا، إذ عرف بذلك أن ثبوت الفعل ليس من مقتضيات ذاتها لازم على هذا ويبطله ولا اعتراض لهم عليه.
Then we say: According to your school, the act is not valid from the capable agent in the first [moment], and in the second he is not described as having power over the act. So the sum of both states is that the act is not valid from him and he has no power over it. How then do you make him a doer (fa'il), indeed a creator (khaliq) of his statement? And that whose existence is not valid and over which there is no power, its existence is inconceivable. Moreover, we have already shown in the discussion regarding power that if its being free of the act were permissible for a time while its essence exists, it would be permissible for [multiple] times, because it is known by that that the existence of the act is not among the necessary concomitants of its essence – this is necessary upon this [view] and invalidates it, and there is no objection for them against it.
Source pages p. 827

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct arguments within the same refutation. First, the author points out a contradiction in the opponent's doctrine: they claim that in the first moment the act is not valid from the agent, and in the second moment he is not described as having power (since power must accompany the act). Therefore, in both moments the agent is effectively incapable, so calling him a 'doer' or 'creator' is incoherent. The second argument reiterates that if power can be free of act at one time, it can be free at all times, because the act is not an essential property of power. This destroys the opponent's position.

Key Terms

فاعل
خالق
خلو
مقتضى
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the opponent's view allows a consistent account of agency despite denying power in the second moment.
Correction: The author shows that denying power in the second moment, combined with denying act in the first, leaves the agent incapable in both, so agency is incoherent.

Checkpoint

A) It is consistent with their principles.
B) It is contradictory because the agent is incapable in both moments.
C) It proves the agent is omnipotent.
D) It shows that power can exist without act.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's view on agency leads to a logical contradiction that leaves the agent incapable in both moments, invalidating any claim of being a doer or creator.

?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila on the Capable Agent Devoid of Action
Refutation of Mu'tazila on the capable agent's relation to action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that a capable agent can be devoid of action, and the rational necessity of act from the capable.

Core Idea
A capable agent cannot be devoid of action; otherwise capability would be meaningless.

The Mu'tazila argued that God can be described as capable even if He does not act, extending this to humans. The author here refutes this by exposing an inconsistency in their doctrine: they end up claiming the agent can neither act nor have power. This passage is a key Maturidi argument establishing the necessary connection between capability and act, using both logical implication and rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila hold that the capable agent can be devoid of action in some states. They claim the act is not valid from the agent in one state, and in another the agent is not described as having power over the act.
This duality is the starting point of the refutation.
2
Follow the exposure of inconsistency
The author combines the two states: if the act is not valid and the agent has no power, then the agent can neither perform the act nor have power over it. Thus he cannot be a doer or creator.
This contradiction undermines their claim.
3
Grasp the argument about power and act
If power could be devoid of act for a moment, it could be devoid for all moments, because act is not a necessary concomitant of power's essence. This implication invalidates the Mu'tazila position.
4
See the absurd consequence and Abu Hashim's equalization
Abu Hashim equalized the two states, allowing the capable agent to never act. The author declares this rationally impossible: every rational person knows a capable agent cannot permanently lack both voluntary motion and stillness.
This destroys the foundations of the Mu'tazila school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من مذهبكم أن الفعل لا يصح من القادر في الأول، وفي الثاني لا يوصف هو بالقدرة على الفعل، فكان مجموع الحالين أنه لا يصح منه الفعل ولا يقدر عليه، فكيف تجعلونه فاعلاً بل خالقًا لقوله؟ وما لا يصح وجوده ولا قدرة عليه لا يتصور وجوده. على أننا بينا من الكلام في جانب القدرة أن خلوها عن الفعل لو جاز وقتًا من مع وجود ذاتها لجاز أوقاتًا، إذ عرف بذلك أن ثبوت الفعل ليس من مقتضيات ذاتها لازم على هذا ويبطله ولا اعتراض لهم عليه. فلما رأى أبو هاشم توجه هذا الإلزام عليهم سوى بين الأمرين، وأجاز خلو القادر - مع توفر آلاته وانعدام الموانع أبد الدهر عن الفعل والترك لا يفعل فعلاً ولا يباشر مأمورًا ولا ضده الذي هو تركه ولا يرتكب منهيًا ولا ضده الذي هو تركه. وهذا مما يعرفه كل عاقل ببديهة عقله أن خلو القادر عن الحركة والسكون الاختياريين محال ممتنع مع هذا هو قول باطل هادم لقواعد مذهبه
From your doctrine: the act is not valid from the capable [agent] in the first [state], and in the second he is not described as having power over the act. So the sum of the two states is that the act is not valid from him nor does he have power over it. How then do you make him a doer, even a creator of his speech? And that whose existence is not valid and over which there is no power cannot be conceived to exist. Moreover, we have shown in the discussion concerning power that if its being devoid of the act were permissible for a time while its essence exists, it would be permissible for [many] times. For it is known thereby that the existence of the act is not a necessary requirement of its essence; this is necessary [i.e., follows] and invalidates it, and there is no objection against them. When Abu Hashim saw this implication directed against them, he equalized the two matters, and allowed the capable [agent] — with his instruments available and obstacles absent, for all eternity — to be devoid of act and refraining: he does not perform an act, nor does he undertake a commanded [act] nor its opposite which is refraining from it, nor does he commit a prohibited [act] nor its opposite which is refraining from it. And this is something every rational person knows by the necessity of his intellect: that the capable [agent] being devoid of voluntary movement and stillness is impossible and absurd. Along with this, it is a false statement that demolishes the foundations of his [i.e., the Mu'tazila] school.
Source pages p. 827

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position on the relationship between the capable agent (al-qādir) and his act. The author begins by exposing an inconsistency in their doctrine: they claim that in one state the act is not valid from the capable agent, and in another state the agent is not described as having power over the act. The conclusion is that the agent can neither perform the act nor have power over it, which makes it impossible to consider him a doer or creator of his speech (referring to the createdness of the Qur'an). The author then presents an argument: if power (al-qudra) could be devoid of act for a single moment while its essence exists, it could be devoid for multiple moments, because the existence of the act is not a necessary concomitant (muqtaḍā) of the essence of power. Abu Hashim (a Mu'tazili leader) saw the force of this implication and equalized the two states, allowing the capable agent to remain forever without performing any act or its opposite (refraining). The author declares this to be obviously false by rational necessity (bi-badīhati ‘aqlīhi), because a capable agent cannot permanently lack both voluntary motion and stillness. Such a position is absurd and destroys the foundations of the Mu'tazila school.

القدرة

أبو هاشم
